            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2013 4:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-03-12"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The wild species of the genus Oryza contain a largely untapped reservoir of agronomically important genes for rice improvement. Here we report the 261-Mb de novo assembled genome sequence of Oryza brachyantha. Low activity of long-terminal repeat retrotransposons and massive internal deletions of ancient long-terminal repeat elements lead to the compact genome of Oryza brachyantha. We model 32,038 protein-coding genes in the Oryza brachyantha genome, of which only 70% are located in collinear positions in comparison with the rice genome. Analysing breakpoints of non-collinear genes suggests that double-strand break repair through non-homologous end joining has an important role in gene movement and erosion of collinearity in the Oryza genomes. Transition of euchromatin to heterochromatin in the rice genome is accompanied by segmental and tandem duplications, further expanded by transposable element insertions. The high-quality reference genome sequence of Oryza brachyantha provides an important resource for functional and evolutionary studies in the genus Oryza. The wild rice species can be used as germplasm resources for this crop&#8217;s genetic improvement. Here Chen and colleagues report the de novo sequencing of the O. brachyanthagenome, and identify the origin of genome size variation, the role of gene movement and its implications on heterochromatin evolution in the rice genome.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

itemprop="address" content="grid.21155.32, 0000 0001 2034 1839, BGI-Shenzhen, Beishan Industrial Zone, Yantian District, Shenzhen, 518083, China" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" King Abdulaziz University, P.O. Box 80200, Jeddah, 21589, Kingdom of Saudi Arabia" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Mingsheng-Chen" data-author-popup="auth-Mingsheng-Chen" data-corresp-id="c3">Mingsheng Chen<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="State Key Laboratory of Plant Genomics, Institute of Genetics and Developmental Biology, Chinese Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.418558.5, 0000 0004 0596 2989, State Key Laboratory of Plant Genomics, Institute of Genetics and Developmental Biology, Chinese Academy of Sciences, No. 1 West Beichen Road, Chaoyang District, Beijing, 100101, China" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The wild species of the genus <i>Oryza</i> contain a largely untapped reservoir of agronomically important genes for rice improvement. Here we report the 261-Mb <i>de novo</i> assembled genome sequence of <i>Oryza brachyantha</i>. Low activity of long-terminal repeat retrotransposons and massive internal deletions of ancient long-terminal repeat elements lead to the compact genome of <i>Oryza brachyantha</i>. We model 32,038 protein-coding genes in the <i>Oryza brachyantha</i> genome, of which only 70% are located in collinear positions in comparison with the rice genome. Analysing breakpoints of non-collinear genes suggests that double-strand break repair through non-homologous end joining has an important role in gene movement and erosion of collinearity in the <i>Oryza</i> genomes. Transition of euchromatin to heterochromatin in the rice genome is accompanied by segmental and tandem duplications, further expanded by transposable element insertions. The high-quality reference genome sequence of <i>Oryza brachyantha</i> provides an important resource for functional and evolutionary studies in the genus <i>Oryza</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Comparative genomics based on the fully sequenced genomes of model organisms have gained insights into gene and genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bennetzen, J. L. . Patterns in grass genome evolution. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 10, 176–181 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d18784e956">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Paterson, A. H., Freeling, M., Tang, H. &amp; Wang, X. . Insights from the comparison of plant genome sequences. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 61, 349–372 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18784e959">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ferguson-Smith, M. A. &amp; Trifonov, V. . Mammalian karyotype evolution. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 950–962 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d18784e962">3</a></sup>. In plants, the genome organization was perplexed by whole-genome duplication, transposable elements and sequence rearrangements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bennetzen, J. L. . Patterns in grass genome evolution. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 10, 176–181 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d18784e966">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d18784e969">4</a></sup>. Even closely related species, such as species in the genus <i>Arabidopsis</i> or <i>Oryza</i>, harbour remarkable fluctuations in genome size, gene number and gene collinearity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18784e979">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18784e982">6</a></sup>. So far, the completely sequenced plant genomes are mostly within a long-range evolutionary timeframe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Paterson, A. H., Freeling, M., Tang, H. &amp; Wang, X. . Insights from the comparison of plant genome sequences. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 61, 349–372 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18784e987">2</a></sup>, thus limiting the ability to deduce the underlying mechanisms for genomic changes. Instead, comparative analysis of orthologous regions or whole-genome sequences within a short evolutionary timeframe, especially within the same genus, brings novel insights into the nature, rate and mechanisms of genome rearrangements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18784e991">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18784e994">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Clark, A. G. et al. Evolution of genes and genomes on the Drosophila phylogeny. Nature 450, 203–218 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18784e997">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Woodhouse, M. R., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. . Transposed genes in Arabidopsis are often associated with flanking repeats. PLoS Genet. 6, e1000949 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1000">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Scannell, D. R., Byrne, K. P., Gordon, J. L., Wong, S. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. . Multiple rounds of speciation associated with reciprocal gene loss in polyploid yeasts. Nature 440, 341–345 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1003">9</a></sup>.</p><p>The genus <i>Oryza</i>, consisting of 24 species along an evolutionary gradient of ~15 million years, is an ideal model for studying plant genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1013">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lu, B. R. . Taxonomy of the genus Oryza (Poaceae): historical perspective and current status. Int. Rice Res. Notes 24, 4–8 (1999) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1016">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tang, L. et al. Phylogeny and biogeography of the rice tribe (Oryzeae): evidence from combined analysis of 20 chloroplast fragments. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 54, 266–277 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1019">12</a></sup>. The evolutionary signatures of <i>Oryza</i> genome evolution vary among different loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1026">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lu, F. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of MONOCULM1-orthologous regions in 14 Oryza genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2071–2076 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1029">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Sanyal, A. et al. Orthologous comparisons of the Hd1 region across genera reveal Hd1 gene lability within diploid Oryza species and disruptions to microsynteny in Sorghum. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 2487–2506 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1032">14</a></sup>, suggesting the demand for whole-genome comparisons of these <i>Oryza</i> species. The wild rice <i>Oryza brachyantha</i> is defined as F genome type and placed on the basal lineage in <i>Oryza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Zou, X. H. et al. Analysis of 142 genes resolves the rapid diversification of the rice genus. Genome Biol. 9, R49 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1045">15</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Note S1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>). It contains a different set of repeat sequences compared with rice or other <i>Oryza</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zuccolo, A. et al. Transposable element distribution, abundance and role in genome size variation in the genus Oryza. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, 152 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1059">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lee, H. R. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation cloning reveals rapid evolutionary patterns of centromeric DNA in Oryza species. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 11793–11798 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1062">17</a></sup>. Its compact genome and unique phylogenetic position put <i>O. brachyantha</i> more close to the ancestral state of the <i>Oryza</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1072">10</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Note S1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S1 and S2</a>). Thus, comparisons of the <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice genomes will provide us a unique opportunity to explore the genomic changes and the underlying mechanisms of <i>Oryza</i> genome evolution.</p><p>We used a whole-genome shotgun approach combined with the bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC)-based physical map to assemble ~261 Mb of the <i>O. brachyantha</i> genome. <i>O. brachyantha</i> has a compact genome composed of less than 30% of repeat elements. We annotated 32,038 gene models in <i>O. brachyantha</i>, which is much lower than in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1101">18</a></sup>, implying a massive amplification of gene families in the domesticated rice genome. We showed that both tandem gene duplications and gene transpositions had contributed to the burst of gene families in the rice genome. These duplicated sequences might have impacts on the erosion of synteny and accumulation of transposable elements in the heterochromatic regions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequence and assembly</h3><p>We used a whole-genome shotgun sequencing approach to generate 31 Gb of the raw sequence of <i>O. brachyantha</i> using the Illumina GA II platform (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S1</a>). The genome was initially assembled using SOAP<i>denovo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1125">19</a></sup>, and the length of the sequence scaffold was further increased by integrating BAC-end sequences generated by Sanger technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, H. et al. Construction, alignment and analysis of twelve framework physical maps that represent the ten genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Biol. 9, R45 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1129">20</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). The final assembled sequence was 261 Mb with a scaffold N50 size of 1.6 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S2</a>). The ordering of the scaffolds along each chromosome was accomplished by integration with the BAC-based physical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, H. et al. Construction, alignment and analysis of twelve framework physical maps that represent the ten genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Biol. 9, R45 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1140">20</a></sup>. The scaffolds were eventually merged into 36 large sequence blocks covering 96% of the sequenced genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). These sequence blocks were anchored onto each chromosome by a cytogenetic approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>), resulting in 12 pseudomolecules representing the 12 chromosomes of <i>O. brachyantha</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Alignment of 36 sequence blocks of O. brachyantha to rice chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Alignment of 36 sequence blocks of <i>O. brachyantha</i> to rice chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="470" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Rice chromosomes are shown on the left, filled with density of repeat elements for every 100 kb window. Sequence blocks of <i>O. brachyantha</i> on each chromosome are represented by black boxes on the right. Syntenic regions, defined as described in Methods, are shaded in grey. Large sequence blocks were anchored to the chromosomes by a cytogenetic approach. Small sequence blocks of pericentromeric regions, in which few collinear genes could be defined, were anchored to the chromosomes by integration with the physical map and confirmation by Southern blot.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Transposable elements in <i>O. brachyantha</i></h3><p>Approximately 29.2% of the <i>O. brachyantha</i> genome is composed of transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S3</a>), lower than rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1197">18</a></sup> (34.8%), sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1201">21</a></sup> (62.0%) and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1205">22</a></sup> (84.2%), consistent with their genome sizes. The <i>Mutator</i>-like element is the most abundant transposon family, accounting for 7.5% (18.3 Mb versus 13.4 Mb in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1213">18</a></sup>) of the <i>O. brachyantha</i> genome and more than 25% of the DNA transposons in <i>O. brachyantha</i>. Retrotransposons, mostly long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, comprise ~10% of the <i>O. brachyantha</i> genome. A total of 184 LTR retrotransposon families have been discovered, including 75 Ty1-<i>copia</i>, 55 Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> and 54 unclassified families. It is interesting to note that 40 families are present in the form of solo LTRs or fragments. The transposable elements are unevenly distributed on each chromosome with retrotransposons concentrated in pericentromeric or heterochromatic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Distributions of genomic features in O. brachyantha and O. sativa on chromosome 4."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Distributions of genomic features in <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> on chromosome 4.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="497" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The two <i>Oryza</i> genomes showed similar distributions of gene and repeat elements: DNA transposons (DNA-TEs) showed a relatively even distribution along the chromosomes, whereas retrotransposons (RTs) were negatively correlated with the distribution of genes. The overall level of RTs was higher in <i>O. sativa</i> than in <i>O. brachyantha</i>, in which the short arms and the proximal regions of the long arms of chromosome 4 showed much obvious contrast. These highly repetitive regions also showed reduced levels of transcription and gene collinearity. The 4',6-diamidino-2-phenylindole-stained pachytene chromosomes for <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> were put along the genomic tracks of chromosome 4. The black arrows indicate the transition region of euchromatin/heterochromatin, whereas the red arrows indicate the centromere positions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">The evolution of genome size in <i>Oryza</i></h3><p>The genomic comparison revealed that only 35% of the <i>O. brachyantha</i> genome was conserved with the rice genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). The genome size variation between the <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice genomes was mainly caused by differences in the lineage-specific evolution of intergenic sequences, of which LTR retrotransposons alone contributed to ~50% of the size difference (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S5 and S6</a>). In <i>O. brachyantha</i>, the amplification of LTR retrotransposons occurred over a relatively long period, with a peak of activity approximately two to three million years ago (MYA; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Only 5.2% of the LTR retrotransposons were amplified more recently (that is, over a period of less than 0.5 MYA). In contrast, nearly 40% of the LTR retrotransposons were inserted into the rice genome within the last 0.5 million years. Consistent with earlier findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tian, Z. et al. Do genetic recombination and gene density shape the pattern of DNA elimination in rice long terminal repeat retrotransposons? Genome Res. 19, 2221–2230 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1311">23</a></sup>, two recent bursts were observed in the rice genome (&lt;0.5 and 1–2 MYA), which together represent 70% of the LTR retrotransposons in rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). These results indicate that massive recent amplifications of LTR retrotransposons, as occurred in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1318">22</a></sup> and sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1322">21</a></sup>, expanded the rice genome in the last two million years.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Dynamic evolution of LTR retrotransposon in O. brachyantha and O. sativa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Dynamic evolution of LTR retrotransposon in <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="768" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Insertion time of LTR retrotransposons in the <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice genomes. For comparison, LTR retrotransposons of rice chromosomes 3 and 8 were annotated using the same method described for <i>O. brachyantha</i>. The insertion times of LTR retrotransposons were estimated from intact LTR retrotransposons as described in Methods. (<b>b</b>) Divergence time of solo LTR families in <i>O. brachyantha</i>. The divergence times of five families with only solo LTR members were estimated as described in Methods.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To counteract the expansion, LTR retrotransposons could be eliminated from the genome through unequal homologous recombination or non-homologous (illegitimate) recombination, resulting in solo LTRs or truncated LTR retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. . Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860–869 (2004) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1371">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. . Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1374">25</a></sup>. The results of higher ratios of solo LTRs and truncated elements to intact LTR elements in <i>O. brachyantha</i> than rice suggests a tendency of shrinkage in <i>O. brachyantha</i> (solo: intact LTR of 1.63 in <i>O. brachyantha</i> versus 0.93 in rice, and truncated: intact LTR of 3.26 in <i>O. brachyantha</i> versus 0.64 in rice). The divergence times of the five solo LTR families indicate that these elements are likely to be ancient families in the genus <i>Oryza</i>, being inactive by deletions, and eventually will be removed from the genome of <i>O. brachyantha</i> by sequence decay (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S4</a>). These results are consistent with recent findings in <i>Arabidopsis</i> that deletion was selectively favoured in a compact genome, in which repression of transposable elements is more efficient<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1406">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hollister, J. D. et al. Transposable elements and small RNAs contribute to gene expression divergence between Arabidopsis thaliana and Arabidopsis lyrata. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 2322–2327 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1409">26</a></sup>. Thus, we conclude that limited recent activity and a massive removal of ancient families through unequal homologous recombination and illegitimate recombination have led to the smaller genome size of <i>O. brachyantha</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolution of gene families in <i>Oryza</i></h3><p>A total of 32,038 protein-coding genes were predicted in <i>O. brachyantha</i> using an evidence-based strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Liang, C., Mao, L., Ware, D. &amp; Stein, L. . Evidence-based gene predictions in plant genomes. Genome Res. 19, 1912–1923 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1431">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). In 18,020 gene families of <i>O. brachyantha</i>, 17,076 (95%) are clustered with rice genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). More than 80% of the gene families shared by <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice have a one-to-one orthologous relationship. Moreover, 1,419 families have a smaller size in <i>O. brachyantha</i>, whereas only 460 families are of a smaller size in rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). Analysis of the Pfam domains indicates that the gene families, such as NB-ARC (<i>P</i>-value ≤1.05 × 10<sup>−5</sup>), Leucine-rich repeat (LRR, <i>P</i>-value≤2.20 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) and F-box (<i>P</i>-value ≤2.20 × 10<sup>−16</sup>), are overrepresented in rice relative to <i>O. brachyantha</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). These disease resistance-related gene families are evolved at a high birth- and death rate in plant genomes, which may reflect its role in adaptation to various environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1480">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jones, J. D. &amp; Dangl, J. L. . The plant immune system. Nature 444, 323–329 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1483">28</a></sup>. Further exploration of gene families of NBS–LRR and RLK–LRR suggests remarkable turnover of family members through gene duplication, transposition and pseudogenization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Freeling, M. et al. Many or most genes in Arabidopsis transposed after the origin of the order Brassicales. Genome Res. 18, 1924–1937 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1487">29</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Tables S5–S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S7–S10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Venn diagram showing the distribution of gene families between O. brachyantha, O. sativa and Sorghum bicolor."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Venn diagram showing the distribution of gene families between <i>O. brachyantha</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="550" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Orthologous gene families are defined in Methods. The numbers of gene families and genes clustered in families are indicated for every species. The intersections between species indicate the numbers of shared gene families, whereas the numbers of unique families are shown in species-specific areas.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Comparison of gene families between O. brachyantha and O. sativa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Comparison of gene families between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="219" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Size variation of gene families. All shared gene families between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, excluding one-to-one orthologues, were compared to show the variation in gene family sizes. Negative values indicate fewer family members in <i>O. sativa</i>, whereas positive values indicate more members in <i>O. sativa</i>. <i>Os</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>; <i>Ob</i>, <i>O. brachyantha</i>. (<b>b</b>) Copy number variation of conserved functional domains. The gene number belonging to each conserved functional domain was retrieved according to Pfam domain annotation (Methods). The <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup>-test, or Fisher’s exact test when the expected frequency was smaller than five, was employed to select significantly different functional domains between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>. Multiple comparisons were corrected by the Bonferroni method as implemented in R. Only significant functional domains with a total number of more than 100 members in two <i>Oryza</i> genomes are shown, ranked by <i>P</i>-value (<i>P</i>-value ≤2 × 10<sup>−4</sup> and <i>q</i>-value ≤0.05).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Conservation of gene organization along chromosomes in <i>Oryza</i></h3><p>The gene organization of <i>Oryza</i> species is highly conserved as demonstrated by regional sequence analysis, although exceptions have been observed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1629">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lu, F. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of MONOCULM1-orthologous regions in 14 Oryza genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2071–2076 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1632">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Sanyal, A. et al. Orthologous comparisons of the Hd1 region across genera reveal Hd1 gene lability within diploid Oryza species and disruptions to microsynteny in Sorghum. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 2487–2506 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1635">14</a></sup>. To reveal the degree and nature for genome organization changes between rice and <i>O. brachyantha</i> separated in evolution for approximately 15 million years, we performed a whole-genome collinearity analysis. Core-orthologous gene pairs were used to define 82 orthologous blocks between the <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice genomes, which covered ~97% (<i>O. brachyantha</i>) and 94% (rice) of predicted gene models. The break intervals between orthologous blocks, including 11 centromeres, were formed by long stretches of nonsyntenic genomic sequences in one or both genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). On the basis of the syntenic blocks, we found 22,405 and 24,103 genes that were conserved in gene collinearity between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice, respectively. These collinear gene pairs formed 19,222 gene clusters, 2,468 of which showed evidence of local gene duplications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). We found many more expanded clusters in rice than that in <i>O. brachyantha</i> (1,363 and 663, respectively). Analysis of functional categories revealed that the duplicated genes were enriched in defence and reproduction process categories, which was consistent with the gene family analysis and suggested significant roles of local duplications in these gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S9</a>). We identified 214 inversions between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S11 and S12</a>). Approximately two-thirds of the inversions were flanked by inverted repeat sequences in one or both genomes; two inversions in the rice genome were found to be linked with the duplication of a flanking gene, revealing a potential novel mechanism for gene duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Furuta, Y. et al. Birth and death of genes linked to chromosomal inversion. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1501–1506 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1674">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ranz, J. M. et al. Principles of genome evolution in the Drosophila melanogaster species group. PLoS Biol. 5, e152 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1677">31</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S13 and S14</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Distribution of sequence rearrangements along the chromosomes of O. sativa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Distribution of sequence rearrangements along the chromosomes of <i>O. sativa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Segmental duplications, tandem duplications, inversions and non-collinear sequence blocks were detected as described in Methods. (<b>a</b>), Donors and acceptors of segmental duplications on rice chromosomes are connected by red lines. (<b>b</b>), Chromosome ideograms in the inner circle are represented by different colours, with chromosome numbers indicated. The three internal heatmaps represent inversions (<b>c</b>), expansion of tandem gene duplications (<b>d</b>) and expansion of non-collinear sequence blocks (<b>e</b>), in which expansion in rice is indicated by red colour and expansion in <i>O. brachyantha</i> indicated by blue colour. Of the 214 inversions between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice genomes, only inversions with more than two collinear genes are shown. Of the 2,460 orthologous tandem gene clusters, 1,378 tandem clusters expanded in the rice genome are shown as red colour and 654 tandem clusters expanded in <i>O. brachyantha</i> are shown as blue colour. Of the non-collinear sequence blocks, only those regions that had expanded more than double in size in rice or <i>O. brachyantha</i> are shown in red or blue, respectively. (<b>f</b>), The densities of genes, RNA retrotransposons and DNA transposons are shown as green, red and orange histograms in the outer circle, respectively. Min–Max: gene (0–25%); RNA retrotransposon (0–80%); DNA transposon (0–80%). Centromeres are indicated by black bars in the outer circle.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Mechanisms on erosion of gene collinearity</h3><p>The degree of gene collinearity in plant genomes tends to decrease with the increase of phylogenetic distance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1750">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1753">32</a></sup>, leaving less than 15% of rice genes collinear with eudicots and ~57% of rice genes collinear with sorghum. However, the underlying mechanism for non-collinear gene formation was not well understood<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Woodhouse, M. R., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. . Transposed genes in Arabidopsis are often associated with flanking repeats. PLoS Genet. 6, e1000949 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1757">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wicker, T., Buchmann, J. P. &amp; Keller, B. . Patching gaps in plant genomes results in gene movement and erosion of colinearity. Genome Res. 20, 1229–1237 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1760">33</a></sup>. We observed more than 30% of genes in <i>O. brachyantha</i> or rice are located in non-collinear positions, with more than half of them supported by homologous proteins or transcriptome data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S11</a>). These non-collinear genes were enriched in pericentromeric or heterochromatic knobs than euchromatic regions in the rice genome, resulting reduced level of gene collinearity in these recombination-inert regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S15 and S16</a>). To reveal the mechanism by which non-collinear genes were created, we introduced an intermediate species <i>Oryza glaberrima</i>, which diverged from rice less than 2 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tang, L. et al. Phylogeny and biogeography of the rice tribe (Oryzeae): evidence from combined analysis of 20 chloroplast fragments. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 54, 266–277 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1777">12</a></sup>. We identified 198 non-collinear genes accumulated in the rice genome posterior to its split with <i>O. glaberrima</i>, including 127 insertions. Forty-five per cent (56 insertions) of them were found to have highly identical homologues in the rice genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S12</a>). By comparison of these 56 trisequence alignments among non-collinear gene regions (acceptor sites), their closest homologous regions (donor sites) and their orthologous regions in <i>Oryza glaberrima</i> (putative ancestral sites), the mechanisms by which the non-collinear genes were created can be revealed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig7">Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S17 and S18</a>). Transposable elements, which have been shown to be frequently involved in gene movements in plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bennetzen, J. L. . Transposable elements, gene creation and genome rearrangement in flowering plants. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 15, 621–627 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1797">34</a></sup>, were found to dominate in 12 insertions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S12</a>). In three cases, we observed that the acceptor sites have long stretches of homologous sequences with the donor sequence, suggesting the insertions were caused by non-allelic homologous recombination during repair of double-strand breaks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Stankiewicz, P. &amp; Lupski, J. R. . Genome architecture, rearrangements and genomic disorders. Trends Genet. 18, 74–82 (2002) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1804">35</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig7">Fig. 7b (III)</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S17c</a>). However, in 41 cases the comparisons revealed no signature of transposable elements or long homologous sequence, but showed microhomology (&lt;10 bp) between the flanking sequences of the acceptor sites and the donor sites (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Table S12</a>). The sequence signatures suggest the insertions were associated with the repair of double-strand breaks through non-homologous recombination, including non-homologous end joining (NHEJ) and microhomology-mediated end joining<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Symington, L. S. &amp; Gautier, J. . Double-strand break end resection and repair pathway choice. Annu. Rev. Genet. 45, 247–271 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1818">36</a></sup>. The breakpoints of these 41 insertions in the rice genome were mostly precise without deletions when compared with <i>O. glaberrima</i>, thus indicating the role of NHEJ in creating these non-collinear genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Symington, L. S. &amp; Gautier, J. . Double-strand break end resection and repair pathway choice. Annu. Rev. Genet. 45, 247–271 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1825">36</a></sup>. The mechanisms of repair of double-strand breaks through non-homologous recombination or non-allelic homologous recombination had important roles in structure variations of human genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kidd, J. M. et al. A human genome structural variation sequencing resource reveals insights into mutational mechanisms. Cell 143, 837–847 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1829">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Mills, R. E. et al. Mapping copy number variation by population-scale genome sequencing. Nature 470, 59–65 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1832">38</a></sup>. Our findings suggest that the repair of double-strand breaks, particularly NHEJ, has a dominant role in gene duplications, and in creating synteny perturbations in plant genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Genomic rearrangements and signature of breakpoints."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7: Genomic rearrangements and signature of breakpoints.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2596/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2596_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1001" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Sequence analysis of a non-collinear gene locus. The region containing an expansion on chromosome 1 of <i>O. sativa</i> was compared with orthologous regions in <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i>. Nearly 17.2 kb of the expansion in <i>O. sativa</i> (acceptor) was formed by an insertion after its split with <i>O. glaberrima</i>. The insertion sequence was highly homologous to a genomic segment on chromosome 9 of <i>O. sativa</i> (donor). Analysis of the breakpoints indicated that the acceptor sequence was a duplicated copy of the donor sequence. (<b>b</b>) Molecular signatures of recently formed non-collinear genes. Red characters, target site duplication; black arrowhead boxes, genes; shaded black arrowhead boxes, pseudogenes; blue boxes, LTR retrotransposons; green boxes, DNA transposons; orange boxes, non-LTR retrotransposons; sequences between square brackets have highly homologous donor sequences; sequences between dashes have no donor sequences. Detailed sequence analyses are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S17 and S18</a>. MULE, <i>Mutator</i>-like element.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2596/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Impact of duplications on the evolution of chromatin</h3><p>Duplicated sequences were found frequently in, but were not restricted to, heterochromatic regions, consistent with their important role in accumulating non-collinear genes in these regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206–13211 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1895">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). Owing to their redundancy, these duplications were more tolerant of mutations, such as transposon insertions and sequence rearrangements, and may therefore act as a hotspot for genome expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lippman, Z. et al. Role of transposable elements in heterochromatin and epigenetic control. Nature 430, 471–476 (2004) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1902">40</a></sup>. If this is the case, we would expect much more expansions in these duplication-rich regions. Indeed, sequence rearrangements were distributed across all of the chromosomes with a particular concentration in the pericentromeric and heterochromatic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S19</a>). In contrast to the collinear regions, the rearranged regions displayed more differences in size between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice, with more regions expanded in rice than in <i>O. brachyantha</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S19</a>). More specifically, we observed a much greater expansion in rice heterochromatic regions H7 and H8 on chromosome 4 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Figs S20 and S21</a>), even with a lower abundance of LTR retrotransposons (29 and 24%). In both regions, gene duplication had a substantial role in genome expansion. Seven tandemly duplicated gene clusters were found in the H8 region, which contributed to ~726 kb expansion in rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S21</a>). Besides tandem duplications, segmental duplications comprised 137 kb of extra sequences in the H7 region of rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig.S20</a>). We also found the rate of retrotransposon accumulation was increased in the duplicated regions of H1 heterochromatic region on chromosome 4, resulting in a very high level of expansion in rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Fig. S22</a>). These results were consistent with the cytogenetic observations that the proximal region of the long arm of rice chromosome 4 were highly heterochromatic, but condense of chromatin were almost undetectable in <i>O. brachyantha</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). The evolutionary fluidity of euchromatin and heterochromatin in orthologous regions between closely related species were also observed in <i>Drosophila</i>, which showed potential influence on gene regulation, implying important roles in species divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yasuhara, J. C., DeCrease, C. H. &amp; Wakimoto, B. T. . Evolution of heterochromatic genes of Drosophila. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 10958–10963 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1954">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Riddle, N. C. et al. Plasticity in patterns of histone modifications and chromosomal proteins in Drosophila heterochromatin. Genome Res. 21, 147–163 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1957">42</a></sup>. The phenomena for transposable element accumulations in duplicated sequences suggest an important role of duplication in genome expansion, and possibly in the formation of heterochromatin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lippman, Z. et al. Role of transposable elements in heterochromatin and epigenetic control. Nature 430, 471–476 (2004) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18784e1961">40</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Comparative analysis of orthologous sequences in heterochromatic domains between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa.</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2596/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Understanding the gene and genome evolution needs both within- and between-species comparisons. The studies in <i>Drosophila</i>, yeast and human lineages demonstrated how comparisons on genome sequences of closely related species revealed mechanisms on gene and genome evolution in animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Clark, A. G. et al. Evolution of genes and genomes on the Drosophila phylogeny. Nature 450, 203–218 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2589">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Scannell, D. R., Byrne, K. P., Gordon, J. L., Wong, S. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. . Multiple rounds of speciation associated with reciprocal gene loss in polyploid yeasts. Nature 440, 341–345 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2592">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Girirajan, S. et al. Sequencing human-gibbon breakpoints of synteny reveals mosaic new insertions at rearrangement sites. Genome Res. 19, 178–190 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2595">43</a></sup>. In plants, <i>Oryza</i> is an excellent system for comparative genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. Dynamic evolution of oryza genomes is revealed by comparative genomic analysis of a genus-wide vertical data set. Plant Cell 20, 3191–3209 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2602">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lu, F. et al. Comparative sequence analysis of MONOCULM1-orthologous regions in 14 Oryza genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2071–2076 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2605">13</a></sup>. The genomic recourses of <i>Oryza</i> were well developed, including BAC libraries and fingerprinted physical maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, H. et al. Construction, alignment and analysis of twelve framework physical maps that represent the ten genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Biol. 9, R45 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2613">20</a></sup>. An international <i>Oryza</i> Map Alignment Project is on the way to generate reference genome sequences for ten representative species in <i>Oryza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Goicoechea, J. L. et al. The future of rice genomics: Sequencing the collective Oryza genome. Rice 3, 89–97 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2622">44</a></sup>. <i>O. brachyantha</i> is of importance because it is one of the most diverged wild rice species and the genome is likely to be more static compared with other <i>Oryza</i> genomes; thus, this provides an opportunity to explore the signatures of gene and genome evolution of <i>Oryza</i> by comparing with the rice genome.</p><p>Taking advantage of the BAC-based physical map, we produced a high-quality genome sequence of <i>O. brachyantha</i>, of which 96% was assembled into 12 pseudo-chromosomes. By manual annotation of the repeat sequences, we demonstrated that the genome of <i>O. brachyantha</i> is more stable with limited activity of transposable element amplifications. The gene number is comparable to that of sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2645">21</a></sup>, <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2651">45</a></sup> and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2655">18</a></sup>. However, through detailed analysis on gene collinearity we found that the rice genome experienced a massive gene amplification after the divergence of <i>Oryza</i>. Besides the contribution of tandem gene duplications, most gene amplifications were caused by gene transpositions that copy and paste genes to non-collinear positions. The gene transposition could be very critical to evolution if the resulting gene evolved towards a novel function or caused interspecies incompatibility. For example, one gene transposition (LOC_OS01g15448, <i>DPL1</i>), caused by non-allelic homologous recombination through double-strand break repair, together with its parental gene (LOC_OS06g08510, <i>DPL2</i>), are responsible for the hybrid incompatibility between <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i> rice through reciprocal gene loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Mizuta, Y., Harushima, Y. &amp; Kurata, N. . Rice pollen hybrid incompatibility caused by reciprocal gene loss of duplicated genes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci.
