USA 107, 20417–20422 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2675">46</a></sup>.</p><p>Several mechanisms were proposed to underlie the formation of non-collinear genes, including capture of gene fragments by transposable elements, retroposition and double-strand break repair<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wicker, T., Buchmann, J. P. &amp; Keller, B. . Patching gaps in plant genomes results in gene movement and erosion of colinearity. Genome Res. 20, 1229–1237 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2682">33</a></sup>. The analysis of this study suggested that NHEJ through double-strand break repair accounts for most cases of gene transpositions in the rice genome. The breakpoints of gene transpositions were precisely determined by comparing the flanking sequences with the donor sites. The repair process needs very few homologous sequences or can occur without homologous sequences, implying even distribution along the chromosomes. However, non-collinear genes were found to be significantly enriched in heterochromatic regions, suggesting that the repression of recombination in heterochromatin might not be efficient in removing non-collinear genes in these regions. The accumulation of duplicated sequences resulted in the complexity of genome organization in heterochromatin. In addition, these redundant sequences are tolerant of transposable element insertions, thus facilitate the accumulation of transposable elements resulting in the transition from euchromatin to heterochromatin. A recent study reported a <i>de novo</i> assembly of the wild ancestor of cultivated rice, <i>O. rufipogon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497–501 (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2691">47</a></sup>. Comparing the genome sequence with the rice genome revealed many functional structure variants located in domestication loci in rice, suggesting possible roles of functional variants in rice domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497–501 (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2695">47</a></sup>.</p><p>In summary, we generated a high-quality <i>de novo</i> reference genome sequence of <i>O. brachyantha</i>. Comparisons with the rice genome revealed mechanisms underlying genome size variation, gene family expansion, gene movement and transition of euchromatin to heterochromatin in the <i>Oryza</i> genomes. Future whole-genome sequencing of the collective <i>Oryza</i> genomes along an evolutionary gradient will render the genus <i>Oryza</i> an unparalleled system for functional and evolutionary studies in plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Sequence and assembly</h3><p>The plants of <i>O. brachyantha</i> (IRGC101232) were kindly provided by the International Rice Research Institute. The nuclear DNA of <i>O. brachyantha</i> was isolated from young leaves using a modified cetyl trimethylammonium bromide protocol, followed by purification using phenol–chloroform. The genomic DNA was fragmented into different sizes to prepare pair-end libraries using standard Illumina protocols. Sequencing was performed on an Illumina Genome Analyzer II. The BAC library and physical map of <i>O. brachyantha</i> was constructed by the <i>Oryza</i> Map Alignment Project at the Arizona Genomics Institute. The sequence assembly was performed with SOAP<i>denovo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2743">19</a></sup>. Reconstruction of pseudo-chromosomes was accomplished by integrating the scaffold sequences with the physical map and confirmed by cytogenetic approaches. Complete details are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene annotation</h3><p>Protein-coding genes were predicted with the Gramene Genebuilder using a strategy of evidence-based gene prediction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Liang, C., Mao, L., Ware, D. &amp; Stein, L. . Evidence-based gene predictions in plant genomes. Genome Res. 19, 1912–1923 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2759">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). FGENESH was used to improve the evidence-based gene models and add further models that had been missed by Gramene Genebuilder. Protein and transcriptional data were collected from various plant species, with a particular focus on monocot species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). The translated coding sequences were obtained from four sequenced monocot species, as well as <i>Poplar</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). The full-length complementary DNAs were obtained mainly from rice (50%) and maize (36%). We also included RNA-seq transcripts from <i>O. brachyantha</i> to improve the accuracy of the gene prediction. Protein-coding genes were annotated by InterProScan to assign Pfam domains and Gene Ontology annotations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. . InterProScan--an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2782">48</a></sup>. Orthologous gene families among <i>O. brachyantha</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> (TIGR6.1) and <i>S. bicolor</i> (v1.4) were identified by OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. . OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2796">49</a></sup> based on BLASTP results with <i>E</i>-values of 10<sup>−5</sup>. The genome assembly, annotation and genome browser can be found at <a href="http://www.gramene.org/Oryza_brachyantha">http://www.gramene.org/Oryza_brachyantha</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Repeat annotation</h3><p>A custom repeat library was developed for <i>O. brachyantha</i> based on structure signatures of different repeat families, as well as homologous searches to known repeat libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>). LTR retrotransposons were detected by screening the genome of <i>O. brachyantha</i> with LTR-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. . LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2829">50</a></sup>. Solo LTRs were discovered by combining analysis of homologous sequence, target site duplication and terminal motif of LTR. Non-LTR retrotransposons and DNA transposons were detected by searching the genome with conserved domains of each repeat family. The candidate sequences were manually checked for the structure signals and target site duplication as described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2596#MOESM410">Supplementary Methods</a>. Classification of subfamilies was based on 80–80–80 rules for LTR retrotransposons and <i>Mutator</i>-like element transposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2840">51</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Estimation of the insertion times for LTR retrotransposons</h3><p>To estimate the insertion times for the full-length LTR retrotransposons, the 5′- and 3′-LTR sequences of the retrotransposons were aligned and used to calculate the <i>K</i>-value (the average number of substitutions per aligned site) using the MEGA 4 programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. . MEGA4: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596–1599 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2855">52</a></sup>. The insertion times (<i>T</i>) were calculated using the formula: <i>T</i>=<i>K</i>/(2 × <i>r</i>), where <i>r</i> represents the average substitution rate, which is 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitutions per synonymous site per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. . Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2877">53</a></sup>. To estimate the divergence date of the retrotransposons that are present in the genome as solo LTRs only, all the intact solo LTRs in the family were used to build a phylogenetic tree and to generate a consensus based on the sequence alignments between the copies of a cluster. All copies of the family were then aligned with the consensus to calculate the <i>K</i>-value. The divergence time was estimated using the same formula and <i>r</i>-value as described above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Tandem duplication and segmental duplication</h3><p>Tandem duplication was defined as neighbouring genes that were not interrupted by more than ten genes. Protein-coding genes were self-searched with <i>E</i>-values ≤10<sup>−5</sup>. The homologous genes were used to construct an undirected graph. Gene pairs in the graph that had a distance of more than ten genes were filtered out. Tandem gene clusters were then retrieved based on resulting connections in the graph. LASTZ was used to identify segmental duplications (length ≥5 kb; identity ≥90%) in <i>O. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Harris, R. S. . Improved Pairwise Alignment Of Genomic DNA PhD thesis, Penn. State Univ. (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2903">54</a></sup>. All self-match alignments were detected by LASTZ based on the repeat-masked genome of <i>O. sativa</i> (<i>K</i>=2,200, <i>L</i>=6,000, <i>Y</i>=3,400, <i>E</i>=30, <i>H</i>=0, <i>O</i>=400, <i>T</i>=1). The original alignments were processed using the Chain/Net package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kent, W. J., Baertsch, R., Hinrichs, A., Miller, W. &amp; Haussler, D. . Evolution's cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc, Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 11484–11489 (2003) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2933">55</a></sup> to chain the well-defined neighbouring alignments. The masked repeat sequences were then reintroduced to the alignments to obtain an optimal global alignment. The candidate segmental duplications (length ≥5 kb; identity ≥90%) were filtered to contain less than 70% repetitive sequences. Genes included in the segmental duplication were obtained by comparing the position of the segmental duplication with the annotated gene.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Interspecies whole-genome alignment</h3><p>The genome sequence of <i>O. brachyantha</i> was masked by RepeatMasker using a custom repeat library created in this study. The genome sequence of <i>O. sativa</i> was masked by RepeatMasker using a rice repeat library. Tandem repeats were masked in both genomes using Tandem Repeat Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Benson, G. . Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2951">56</a></sup>. The whole-genome alignment between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> was constructed on masked genomes using LASTZ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Harris, R. S. . Improved Pairwise Alignment Of Genomic DNA PhD thesis, Penn. State Univ. (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2962">54</a></sup> with parameters: <i>K</i>=2,200, <i>L</i>=6,000, <i>Y</i>=3,400, <i>E</i>=30, <i>H</i>=0, <i>O</i>=400, <i>T</i>=1. Post-processing was performed using the Chain/Net package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kent, W. J., Baertsch, R., Hinrichs, A., Miller, W. &amp; Haussler, D. . Evolution's cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc, Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 11484–11489 (2003) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d18784e2988">55</a></sup> with custom Perl scripts, resulting in a set of orthologous alignments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene collinearity and sequence rearrangements</h3><p>The syntenic blocks between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> were defined by MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3006">4</a></sup> based on core-orthologous gene sets identified using InParanoid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="O'Brien, K. P., Remm, M. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. . Inparanoid: a comprehensive database of eukaryotic orthologs. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D476–D480 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3010">57</a></sup> (BLAST <i>E</i>-value ≤10<sup>−5</sup>; number of genes required to call synteny ≥5). The syntenic blocks were confirmed to represent the orthologous blocks between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa.</i> Genes were then classified as collinear or non-collinear according to whether they have a homologous gene in the orthologous regions. If a homologous gene was not detected in the syntenic region of the target genome, we would search for homologous DNA sequences of the candidate gene in this region and syntenic status would be assigned ‘without synteny status’ for this gene when sequence remnants was detected, which means the orthologous gene was probably missannotated and the synteny status of this gene is not sure. To minimize the influence of sequence gaps on synteny analysis, we manually inspected the gap-containing genes and gap-flanking genes to confirm their syntney status and incorporate the result into synteny analysis. We also used sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3026">21</a></sup>, <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3032">45</a></sup> and foxtail millet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Bennetzen, J. L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3037">58</a></sup> as outgroups to filter these candidate non-collinear genes that were collinear with outgroups. The same procedure was performed between <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i> genomes, which was provided by Dr Rod Wing, to get the most recently formed non-collinear genes in the rice genome. A collinear region is described as a region that contains only collinear genes in both genomes, whereas a rearranged region is described as a region that contains only non-collinear genes or sequence arrangements, such as inversions, in both genomes. Inversions are defined as a region or a cluster of genes that is shared between <i>O. brachyantha</i> and rice, but in the reverse direction. The 10-kb sequences flanking inversions were compared by SSEARCH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Pearson, W. R. . Searching protein sequence libraries: comparison of the sensitivity and selectivity of the Smith-Waterman and FASTA algorithms. Genomics 11, 635–650 (1991) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3050">59</a></sup> to find inverted repeats between the upstream and downstream sequences of inversions (<i>E</i>-value ≤0.01), which could cause inversions by homologous recombination. Gene expressions of duplicated genes were obtained from the Rice MPSS database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Nakano, M. et al. Plant MPSS databases: signature-based transcriptional resources for analyses of mRNA and small RNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D731–D735 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms2596#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d18784e3058">60</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec19" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec19-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec19">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec19-content"><p>Accession codes: The raw reads for this project have been deposited in the NCBI SRA project under the accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA046388">SRA046388</a>. The Illumina reads can be accessed under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX099337">SRX099337</a> to <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX099351">SRX099351</a> for whole-genome shotgun reads and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX100097">SRX100097</a> to <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX100098">SRX100098</a> for RNA-seq reads. The genome assembly has been deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AGAT00000000">AGAT00000000</a>. The version described in this paper is the first version, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AGAT01000000">AGAT01000000</a>.</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Chen, J. <i>et al</i>. Whole-genome sequencing of <i>Oryza brachyantha</i> reveals mechanisms underlying <i>Oryza</i> genome evolution. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 4:1595 doi: 10.1038/ncomms2596 (2013).</p></div></div></section>

M.-S.C., J.W., R.A.W. and J.-Y.W. designed and coordinated the project. B.L., L.Y. and J.-F.S. prepared the materials. Q.-F.H., B.W., B.-H.L., Y.H., Z.-Y.L., Y.C., Q.-L.C., J.-M.M., J.-J.L., Y.Z. and Y.Y. performed sequencing and assembly. J.L.G. and R.A.W. generated and analysed the fingerprinted physical map. T.-Y.L., C.-B.C., W.-L.Z., W.-Q.S. and J.-M.J. performed the cytogenetic experiments. J.-F.C., B.L., Y.L., X.-M.Z. and M.-J.W performed reconstruction of pseudo-chromosomes. J.-F.C., C.-Z.L. and S.-H.S. performed gene annotation. D.-Y.G. and S.A.J. performed repeat annotation. J.-F.C., Y.-S.L., Z.-T.B., G.L., H.-L.Z., Q.-L.Y., N.A., H.-L.W. and C.-L.S performed comparative analysis. J.-F.C. and M.-S.C. wrote the paper.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
